I‚Äôm checking the updated draft as a whole now, not just the new insertions. The main questions are whether the W=0W=0 side is now honestly reduced the way we intended, whether there are still duplicated/conditional statements hiding elsewhere, and what the single narrowest remaining theorem is.

Holistically: the architecture is now in the right place, and the important new reductions are captured.

What is now genuinely in the draft is the right split of the geometry. The W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 side is no longer treated as one vague obstruction: open-interval flatness is excluded under the current direct septic hypothesis via the affine-dependence contradiction, and finite-order isolated W=0W=0 boundaries are locally sterile for pair-pair birth. The turning-boundary side is also sharper now: you have local support-overlap truncation and the directional-kernel bridge, so the turning problem has been reduced from ‚Äúmysterious local geometry‚Äù to a compact interior kernel problem. That is a real structural improvement, and it matches the direction the other thread had converged to.

But no, not everything needed for a full proof is captured yet.

The narrowest remaining gaps now look like this.

First, the geometric closure is still not done. The current draft only gets you from an adjacent pair-pair line to a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels. It does not yet convert that into the one-sided kernel theorem the rest of the turning-boundary stack wants. In other words, the geometry has been reduced to the directional-kernel / zero-capture problem, but that problem is still open. Your own current bottleneck/state remarks now basically say that, and I think they are correct to do so.

Second, the local algebra is still not fully closed. The remaining audited list is now much smaller, but it is still real: first-order surjectivity, quintic pure-value trace annihilation or a substitute detector, the full total septic fixed-sector pair (u7,v7)(u7‚Äã,v7‚Äã), and the direct septic witness as a standalone local theorem. Those are no longer sprawling unknowns, but they are still genuine missing pieces.

Third, the endgame still overstates the proof status. The draft now honestly says in its WIP remark that the contradiction theorem is written in the pair-like branch and that the genuinely finite-core branch is not finished, but the theorem statement still says ‚ÄúConsequently the Riemann Hypothesis holds.‚Äù That remains stronger than the proved architecture currently warrants. The draft is coherent as a work-in-progress proof program; it is not yet internally aligned as a finished proof document.

So my honest summary is:

    as a research draft / architecture: yes, the important new picture is now captured;

    as a complete proof: no, the last serious theorem stack is still open.

The next plan of attack should now be much narrower than before.

I would stop spending time on the W=0W=0 side unless a new obstruction appears. That front is now sufficiently reduced:
finite-order W=0W=0 is locally sterile, and W‚â°0W‚â°0 on an M‚âÝ0MÓÄÝ=0 region is excluded under the current direct-septic hypothesis. The main battle is not there anymore.

The next attack should be:

1. Symmetrize the turning-boundary kernel machinery.
Right now your bridge lemma yields a compact interior zero of either K21K21‚Äã or K12K12‚Äã. The clean next theorem is not to force orientation immediately, but to build the compact first-zero / first-zero-implies-tangency argument for both directional kernels. That avoids needing a premature orientation theorem.

Concretely, I would prove the following pair of lemmas in parallel on compact interior subintervals J1‚ãêI1J1‚Äã‚ãêI1‚Äã, J2‚ãêI2J2‚Äã‚ãêI2‚Äã:
K21(s,t)=det‚Å°(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),K12(t,s)=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)).
K21‚Äã(s,t)=det(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),K12‚Äã(t,s)=det(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)).

For each kernel separately:

    compact first-zero realization;

    first-zero implies tangency by the implicit function theorem;

    tangency contradicts the local support-ordering / endpoint exclusion already known near the turning boundary.

Once that is in place, the ‚Äúdirectional kernel orientation‚Äù issue may simply disappear, because you will not care which direction the overlap endpoint selects.

That is the single best next theorem stack.

2. Only after that, close the global no-four-secant theorem through support transitivity.
Once one of the directional kernels is ruled out on every adjacent pair, the support-order argument becomes the final geometric closure. This is the stage where the current draft still says ‚Äúif adjacent sign regularity is available, the global no-secant theorem follows.‚Äù That implication is no longer the main problem; the adjacent directional-kernel closure is.

3. Then return to the local algebraic audits in this order:
first the quintic pure-value trace issue, then the full total septic fixed-sector pair, then the first-order surjectivity cleanup. The reason for that order is practical: the quintic pure-value point is the smallest local theorem that affects the first residual layer directly, while the full septic pair is the largest remaining algebraic package.

So the shortest ‚Äúnext plan‚Äù is:

    freeze the current W=0W=0 reductions;

    prove a symmetrized compact directional-kernel exclusion theorem at generic turning boundaries;

    derive the global no-four-secant consequence from that;

    then finish the remaining local algebra.

    K21(s,t)=det‚Å°(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),K12(t,s)=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s))

Conditional symmetrized directional-kernel exclusion theorem

Let I1<I2I1‚Äã<I2‚Äã be adjacent ECT intervals across a generic turning boundary
M(hb)=0,M‚Ä≤(hb)‚âÝ0,N(hb)‚âÝ0.
M(hb‚Äã)=0,M‚Ä≤(hb‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0,N(hb‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0.

Define
K21(s,t):=det‚Å°(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),s‚ààI2, t‚ààI1,
K21‚Äã(s,t):=det(F(s),F‚Ä≤(s),F(t)),s‚ààI2‚Äã, t‚ààI1‚Äã,

and
K12(t,s):=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),t‚ààI1, s‚ààI2,
K12‚Äã(t,s):=det(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),t‚ààI1‚Äã, s‚ààI2‚Äã,

with F=(c,v5,Œî7)F=(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã).

Assume the two directional zero-capture hypotheses:

    every zero of K21K21‚Äã on I2√óI1I2‚Äã√óI1‚Äã lies in some compact interior rectangle J2√óJ1‚äÇI2√óI1J2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã‚äÇI2‚Äã√óI1‚Äã;

    every zero of K12K12‚Äã on I1√óI2I1‚Äã√óI2‚Äã lies in some compact interior rectangle J1‚Ä≤√óJ2‚Ä≤‚äÇI1√óI2J1‚Ä≤‚Äã√óJ2‚Ä≤‚Äã‚äÇI1‚Äã√óI2‚Äã.

Then
K21(s,t)‚âÝ0(s‚ààI2, t‚ààI1),
K21‚Äã(s,t)ÓÄÝ=0(s‚ààI2‚Äã, t‚ààI1‚Äã),

and
K12(t,s)‚âÝ0(t‚ààI1, s‚ààI2).
K12‚Äã(t,s)ÓÄÝ=0(t‚ààI1‚Äã, s‚ààI2‚Äã).

So under symmetric zero-capture, both directional kernels are excluded across a generic turning boundary.
Why this is the right theorem

Once you have this, the bridge lemma from adjacent pair-pair overlap to a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels becomes enough. You no longer need a separate orientation theorem selecting ‚Äúthe right branch tangent‚Äù in advance. Whatever direction the overlap endpoint chooses, that kernel is forbidden.

That is a real simplification.
Proof skeleton for K21K21‚Äã

This is basically the turning-boundary theorem you already have, but stated cleanly as conditional on zero-capture.

Assume K21K21‚Äã has some zero. By endpoint-tangent exclusion, there is a boundary-near region where K21‚âÝ0K21‚ÄãÓÄÝ=0. By the K21K21‚Äã zero-capture hypothesis, all zeros lie in a compact interior rectangle J2√óJ1J2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã. Then compact first-zero reduction gives a zero
(s‚àó,t‚àó)‚ààJ2√óJ1
(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)‚ààJ2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã

whose ss-coordinate is boundary-nearest in the x=v5/cx=v5‚Äã/c ordering. The existing first-zero-implies-tangency step then yields
K21(s‚àó,t‚àó)=0,‚àÇtK21(s‚àó,t‚àó)=0.
K21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)=0,‚àÇt‚ÄãK21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)=0.

So the tangent line at Œì(s‚àó)Œì(s‚àó‚Äã) is also tangent at Œì(t‚àó)Œì(t‚àó‚Äã). That gives a common tangent slope across the adjacent branches.

Now there are only two possibilities.

If both tangencies are interior, this contradicts adjacent slope-range separation at a generic turning boundary. If one tangency is at the boundary endpoint slope, this contradicts endpoint-tangent exclusion. Therefore no such zero exists.

That is exactly the current one-sided argument, just honestly packaged as conditional. The draft/convo already has this structure and already identifies zero-capture as the live missing theorem.

Yes ‚Äî I think this attack works.

The key point is that the current bridge from adjacent pair-pair overlap only gives a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels, while the present turning-boundary theorem only treats the K21K21‚Äã direction and is explicitly conditional on zero-capture. So the right next step is exactly to mirror the first-zero/tangency argument for K12K12‚Äã and then state a symmetric conditional exclusion theorem.

Here is the clean math.
1. The mirrored first-zero lemma

Define
K12(t,s):=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),t‚ààI1, s‚ààI2,
K12‚Äã(t,s):=det(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),t‚ààI1‚Äã, s‚ààI2‚Äã,

with
F(h)=(c(h),v5(h),Œî7(h)).
F(h)=(c(h),v5‚Äã(h),Œî7‚Äã(h)).

Assume J1‚ãêI1J1‚Äã‚ãêI1‚Äã, J2‚ãêI2J2‚Äã‚ãêI2‚Äã are compact interior subintervals and the zero set of K12K12‚Äã in J1√óJ2J1‚Äã√óJ2‚Äã is nonempty. Choose a zero
(t‚àó,s‚àó)‚ààJ1√óJ2
(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)‚ààJ1‚Äã√óJ2‚Äã

whose tt-coordinate is minimal in the x=v5/cx=v5‚Äã/c ordering on J1J1‚Äã.

Then the mirrored first-zero statement should be:
‚àÇsK12(t‚àó,s‚àó)=0.
‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)=0.
Proof

Suppose instead that
‚àÇsK12(t‚àó,s‚àó)‚âÝ0.
‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0.

Then by the implicit function theorem there is a C1C1 zero-curve
s=s(t)
s=s(t)

through (t‚àó,s‚àó)(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã) on some open interval t‚àà(t‚àó‚àíŒµ,t‚àó+Œµ)t‚àà(t‚àó‚Äã‚àíŒµ,t‚àó‚Äã+Œµ). Because J1‚ãêI1J1‚Äã‚ãêI1‚Äã is compact interior and t‚àót‚àó‚Äã is chosen as the first zero in the tt-ordering, we may arrange J1J1‚Äã so that t‚àót‚àó‚Äã is not trapped at the left endpoint of the compact box: there is room inside J1J1‚Äã for t<t‚àót<t‚àó‚Äã arbitrarily close to t‚àót‚àó‚Äã.

But then for t<t‚àót<t‚àó‚Äã sufficiently close, the point (t,s(t))(t,s(t)) is also a zero of K12K12‚Äã, contradicting the minimality of t‚àót‚àó‚Äã.

Hence
‚àÇsK12(t‚àó,s‚àó)=0.
‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)=0.

Now
K12(t,s)=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),‚àÇsK12(t,s)=det‚Å°(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F‚Ä≤(s)).
K12‚Äã(t,s)=det(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F(s)),‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t,s)=det(F(t),F‚Ä≤(t),F‚Ä≤(s)).

So at (t‚àó,s‚àó)(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã),
K12(t‚àó,s‚àó)=0,‚àÇsK12(t‚àó,s‚àó)=0
K12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)=0,‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)=0

means both F(s‚àó)F(s‚àó‚Äã) and F‚Ä≤(s‚àó)F‚Ä≤(s‚àó‚Äã) lie in the plane spanned by F(t‚àó),F‚Ä≤(t‚àó)F(t‚àó‚Äã),F‚Ä≤(t‚àó‚Äã). In the affine chart, that says:

    Œì(s‚àó)Œì(s‚àó‚Äã) lies on the tangent line at Œì(t‚àó)Œì(t‚àó‚Äã),

    and the tangent direction at s‚àós‚àó‚Äã is along that same line.

So the tangent line at Œì(t‚àó)Œì(t‚àó‚Äã) is also tangent at Œì(s‚àó)Œì(s‚àó‚Äã). This is exactly the left-right mirror of the existing K21K21‚Äã first-zero lemma. Compare the current one-sided first-zero/tangency mechanism already in the draft/convo.
2. Symmetric conditional exclusion theorem

Now assume I1<I2I1‚Äã<I2‚Äã are adjacent ECT intervals across a generic turning boundary
M(hb)=0,M‚Ä≤(hb)‚âÝ0,N(hb)‚âÝ0.
M(hb‚Äã)=0,M‚Ä≤(hb‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0,N(hb‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0.

Assume symmetric zero-capture:

    every zero of K21K21‚Äã on I2√óI1I2‚Äã√óI1‚Äã is captured in a compact interior rectangle J2√óJ1J2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã;

    every zero of K12K12‚Äã on I1√óI2I1‚Äã√óI2‚Äã is captured in a compact interior rectangle J1‚Ä≤√óJ2‚Ä≤J1‚Ä≤‚Äã√óJ2‚Ä≤‚Äã.

Then both kernels are zero-free:
K21(s,t)‚âÝ0(s‚ààI2, t‚ààI1),
K21‚Äã(s,t)ÓÄÝ=0(s‚ààI2‚Äã, t‚ààI1‚Äã),

and
K12(t,s)‚âÝ0(t‚ààI1, s‚ààI2).
K12‚Äã(t,s)ÓÄÝ=0(t‚ààI1‚Äã, s‚ààI2‚Äã).
Proof for K21K21‚Äã

This is the current conditional adjacent theorem: boundary-near exclusion, compact first-zero, first-zero-implies-tangency, then contradiction with adjacent slope-range separation / endpoint tangent exclusion. That structure is already in place.
Proof for K12K12‚Äã

Run the mirrored argument.

If K12K12‚Äã has a zero, then by the zero-capture hypothesis all its zeros lie in a compact interior box J1‚Ä≤√óJ2‚Ä≤J1‚Ä≤‚Äã√óJ2‚Ä≤‚Äã. By the same compact-first-zero reduction, choose a zero (t‚àó,s‚àó)(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã) whose tt-coordinate is minimal in the x=v5/cx=v5‚Äã/c ordering on the left branch.

By the mirrored lemma above,
‚àÇsK12(t‚àó,s‚àó)=0.
‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)=0.

So the tangent line at Œì(t‚àó)Œì(t‚àó‚Äã) is also tangent at Œì(s‚àó)Œì(s‚àó‚Äã). That gives a common tangent line between the adjacent branches.

Now two possibilities:

    if both tangencies are interior, this contradicts adjacent slope-range separation at a generic turning boundary;

    if the common tangent is the boundary endpoint tangent, this contradicts endpoint tangent exclusion at the turning boundary.

So no such zero exists. Hence K12‚âÝ0K12‚ÄãÓÄÝ=0 everywhere as well.

That closes the symmetric conditional theorem.
3. Why this is enough for adjacent pair-pair exclusion

The draft‚Äôs newer bridge lemma already reduces any adjacent pair-pair line across a generic turning boundary to a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels, not necessarily K21K21‚Äã. That was exactly the directional-orientation issue. Once the symmetric theorem above is proved, the issue disappears: whichever directional kernel the overlap endpoint selects is forbidden. That is why this is the right next theorem.

So the logical chain becomes:

    adjacent pair-pair line

    ‚áí‚áí compact interior zero of one of K21,K12K21‚Äã,K12‚Äã

    ‚áí‚áí contradiction by symmetric conditional exclusion.

4. What remains after this

This still does not prove the geometry unconditionally. It only moves the final geometric gap into a cleaner form:

    prove zero-capture for both directional kernels on adjacent intervals across a generic turning boundary.

That is now the exact remaining turning-boundary theorem. It is narrower and better posed than the old ‚Äúprove adjacent tangent-separator sign regularity‚Äù ask. The convo already identified zero-capture as the clean bottleneck; this just makes the bottleneck symmetric and removes the separate orientation annoyance.

So my honest read is:

    the mirrored first-zero lemma is sound;

    the symmetric conditional exclusion theorem should go through cleanly;

    after that, the only remaining global-geometric task is a symmetric zero-capture proposition.

The next exact thing to write is the mirrored lemma in paper notation, then a theorem:

    Adjacent directional-kernel exclusion across a generic turning boundary, conditional on symmetric zero-capture.

That is the sharpest forward move now.

This is good. I think the **mirrored first-zero lemma is basically sound**, and the **symmetric conditional exclusion theorem is the right next theorem**.

There are two places I would tighten before you hand it back.

The first is in the mirrored lemma proof. The sentence

> ‚Äúwe may arrange (J_1) so that (t_*) is not trapped at the left endpoint of the compact box‚Äù

is the weak point. You do not need that phrasing, and it invites a criticism.

A cleaner version is:

* choose (t_*) minimal in the (x=v_5/c) ordering among **all zeros of (K_{12}) in (I_1\times I_2)**;
* zero-capture then tells you this zero lies in a compact interior rectangle (J_1\times J_2);
* because (J_1\Subset I_1), the point (t_*) is an interior point of the ambient interval (I_1), so there are nearby (t<t_*) still in (I_1);
* if (\partial_sK_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq0), the implicit function theorem gives a local zero curve (s=s(t)) through ((t_*,s_*)), hence zeros with (t<t_*) arbitrarily close to (t_*), contradicting minimality.

That avoids talking about the left edge of the compact box. The contradiction is with minimality in the **ambient domain**, not with the box geometry. The compact box is only there to guarantee the selected zero is interior enough for the implicit-function continuation. 

The second thing I would make explicit is the boundary-near exclusion for (K_{12}). For (K_{21}), the draft already has the one-sided turning-boundary theorem. For (K_{12}), the same exclusion should now come from the newer local support-ordering theorem at a generic turning boundary:

[
\phi_m(h)=\frac{\Delta_7(h)-m v_5(h)}{c(h)}
]
has
[
\phi_m'(h_b)=\frac{N(h_b)}{c(h_b)^2}\neq 0
]
for every finite slope (m), and (x=v_5/c) has a strict local extremum, so both finite-slope and vertical directions are locally ordered. That is the clean local reason a tangent from one side cannot meet the other branch arbitrarily near the turning boundary, in either orientation. I would say that explicitly rather than just ‚Äúby the same endpoint-tangent exclusion logic.‚Äù 

So my verdict is:

* **mirrored first-zero lemma:** yes, sound after the minimality sentence is cleaned up;
* **symmetric conditional exclusion theorem:** yes, this is the right theorem;
* **remaining real gap:** still symmetric zero-capture, not the mirrored tangency argument itself. 

The version I would hand back is:

> Choose ((t_*,s_*)) with (t_*) minimal in the (x=v_5/c) ordering among all zeros of (K_{12}) on (I_1\times I_2). By zero-capture, ((t_*,s_*)) lies in a compact interior rectangle. If (\partial_sK_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq0), the implicit function theorem gives a local zero curve (s=s(t)), producing zeros with boundary-nearer (t), contradiction. Hence (\partial_sK_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0). Then (K_{12}=0) and (\partial_sK_{12}=0) mean the tangent at (\Gamma(t_*)) is also tangent at (\Gamma(s_*)), contradicting adjacent interior slope-range separation or endpoint tangent exclusion.

So I would tell the other chat: **the theorem works; just rewrite the minimality/IFT step in ambient-domain language and make the (K_{12}) boundary-near exclusion explicitly rest on the new local support-ordering theorem.**

(t‚àó,s‚àó)

with t‚àót‚àó‚Äã minimal in the x=v5/cx=v5‚Äã/c ordering among all zeros of K12K12‚Äã on I1√óI2I1‚Äã√óI2‚Äã.

Proof

‚àÇs‚ÄãK12‚Äã(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0.

Then by the implicit function theorem there is a C1C1 local zero curve
s=s(t)
s=s(t)

through (t‚àó,s‚àó)(t‚àó‚Äã,s‚àó‚Äã), defined for tt near t‚àót‚àó‚Äã, such that
K12(t,s(t))=0.
K12‚Äã(t,s(t))=0.

Make the K12K12‚Äã boundary-near exclusion explicit

I would state this separately, because it is now a genuine local theorem and not just ‚Äúsame logic as before.‚Äù

At a generic turning boundary, for every finite slope mm,
œïm(h)=Œî7(h)‚àímv5(h)c(h)
œïm‚Äã(h)=c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚àímv5‚Äã(h)‚Äã

satisfies
œïm‚Ä≤(hb)=N(hb)c(hb)2‚âÝ0.
œïm‚Ä≤‚Äã(hb‚Äã)=c(hb‚Äã)2N(hb‚Äã)‚ÄãÓÄÝ=0.

So for each fixed finite slope mm, œïmœïm‚Äã is strictly monotone on both sides near the boundary. Also x=v5/cx=v5‚Äã/c has a strict local extremum there, so the vertical direction is locally ordered too.

Therefore no tangent line from one side can meet the other branch arbitrarily near the boundary, in either orientation. That gives the needed boundary-near zero exclusion for both directional kernels K21K21‚Äã and K12K12‚Äã.

That is the clean local input.

\begin{lemma}[Boundary-near exclusion for both directional kernels at a generic turning boundary]
\label{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Define
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t)),
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\]
with
\[
F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h)).
\]
Then there is a neighborhood of the boundary pair on which both directional
kernels are nonzero:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0,
\qquad
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\]
for \(s\in I_2\), \(t\in I_1\) sufficiently close to \(h_b\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Write
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
Since
\[
y'(h)=\frac{N(h)}{c(h)^2},
\]
the hypothesis \(N(h_b)\neq 0\) implies that \(y\) is a valid local
coordinate near \(h_b\). Thus the normalized planar curve may be written
locally as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y).
\]
At \(h_b\),
\[
\frac{dx}{dy}=\frac{M(h)}{N(h)},
\]
so
\[
\left.\frac{dx}{dy}\right|_{h_b}=0.
\]
Differentiating once more,
\[
\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}
=
\frac{c(h)^2}{N(h)}\cdot\frac{M'(h)N(h)-M(h)N'(h)}{N(h)^2},
\]
and since \(M(h_b)=0\),
\[
\left.\frac{d^2x}{dy^2}\right|_{h_b}
=
\frac{c(h_b)^2\,M'(h_b)}{N(h_b)^2}\neq 0.
\]
Hence, after shrinking to a small neighborhood of \(h_b\), the graph
\(x=\chi(y)\) is strictly convex or strictly concave.

For such a graph, the tangent line at any point meets the graph only at its
point of tangency. Therefore no tangent line from one side can meet the
opposite side inside a sufficiently small neighborhood of the boundary.
Equivalently, both directional tangent-separator kernels are nonzero near the
boundary pair.
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Mirrored first adjacent zero implies tangency]
\label{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact interior subintervals
across a generic turning boundary, and define
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s)),
\qquad
t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2.
\]
Assume every zero of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in
\(J_1\times J_2\). Let \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) be a zero whose
\(t\)-coordinate is minimal in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of
\(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\). Then
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Because \(M\neq 0\) on the open interval \(I_1\), the function
\[
x(h)=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)}
\]
is a valid local coordinate on \(I_1\). Since every zero of \(K_{12}\) lies in
the compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\), the chosen point
\((t_*,s_*)\) is interior in the ambient domain \(I_1\times I_2\).

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\) is interior in \(I_1\), there are nearby points with smaller
\(x=v_5/c\) ordering still in \(I_1\). For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Conditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion across turning boundaries]
\label{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Assume:
\begin{enumerate}
\item the zero-capture hypothesis required in
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity} for the kernel
\(K_{21}\) on \(I_2\times I_1\);
\item the symmetric zero-capture hypothesis for \(K_{12}\), namely that every
zero of
\[
K_{12}(t,s)=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))
\]
on \(I_1\times I_2\) lies in some compact interior rectangle
\(J_1\times J_2\Subset I_1\times I_2\).
\end{enumerate}
Then both directional kernels are zero-free:
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad (s\in I_2,\ t\in I_1),
\]
and
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad (t\in I_1,\ s\in I_2).
\]
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
The first assertion is exactly
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-tangent-separator-sign-regularity}.

For the second assertion, boundary-near exclusion for \(K_{12}\) is provided by
Lemma~\ref{lem:boundary-near-directional-kernel-exclusion}. Assume for
contradiction that \(K_{12}\) has a zero. By the symmetric zero-capture
hypothesis, every zero lies in a compact interior rectangle \(J_1\times J_2\).
Choose a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) whose \(t\)-coordinate is minimal
in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all zeros of \(K_{12}\) on \(I_1\times I_2\).

By Lemma~\ref{lem:mirrored-first-adjacent-zero-implies-tangency},
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0,
\]
this implies that the tangent line at \(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\). Hence the two adjacent intervals share a common interior
tangent slope, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}. Therefore
\(K_{12}\) has no zero.
\end{proof}

\begin{remark}[Status of the directional-kernel reduction]
\label{rem:status-directional-kernel-reduction}
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero} reduces an adjacent
pair-pair line across a generic turning boundary to a compact interior zero of
one of the two directional tangent-separator kernels. Theorem~\ref{thm:conditional-symmetric-directional-kernel-exclusion}
shows that the remaining turning-boundary issue is no longer orientation
selection between \(K_{21}\) and \(K_{12}\), but the symmetric zero-capture
problem for these two kernels.
\end{remark}

I would not assume we can just close symmetric zero-capture from the current ingredients.

My honest read is: probably not as a clean theorem of the form ‚Äúall zeros of both directional kernels lie in a compact interior box‚Äù. The current draft/convo still treats zero-capture as the live turning-boundary theorem, and nothing in the latest push obviously removes the core difficulty that a zero sequence could escape toward a non-common endpoint of I1I1‚Äã or I2I2‚Äã, not just toward the shared boundary. That is exactly why the current turning-boundary theorem was made conditional in the first place.

What is now in place is stronger local control:

    finite-order W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundaries are locally sterile for pair-pair birth;

    generic turning boundaries have local support ordering / boundary-near exclusion;

    pair-pair overlap across a generic turning boundary yields a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels;

    the mirrored first-zero lemma and the symmetric conditional theorem reduce the remaining issue to symmetric zero-capture for K21K21‚Äã and K12K12‚Äã.

So the aggressive thing to do now is not try to prove full symmetric zero-capture in one jump. I think the better move is to attack a theorem that is both weaker and more closely tailored to the global obstruction.

The right replacement target is:

    pair-pair-generated zero-capture, not full zero-capture.

In other words:

If a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary, then the directional-kernel zero produced by the compact support-overlap endpoint argument is already realized on a compact interior pair, and the first-zero/tangency contradiction can be run inside that compact overlap family, without needing a theorem about all other zeros of the kernel on the whole open rectangle.

That avoids the hardest part of zero-capture entirely: you no longer need to control arbitrary stray zeros near far endpoints that may be irrelevant to the pair-pair obstruction.

So my blunt answer is:

    Can we aggressively attack symmetric zero-capture? Yes.

    Do I think we can cleanly close the full theorem right now? No, not from the current ingredients alone.

    Do I think we can likely bypass full zero-capture with a more targeted compact first-contact theorem tied to support overlap? Yes, and that is where I would push next.

The theorem I would try to prove next is this:

    Let I1<I2I1‚Äã<I2‚Äã be adjacent across a generic turning boundary. Suppose a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both branches. Then, after the support-overlap truncation already in the draft, there exists a compact interior overlap family for which the overlap endpoint produces a directional kernel zero that is first in the relevant branch-ordering within that compact family, and hence yields the tangency contradiction.

If that works, you do not need global zero-capture for all kernel zeros. You only need a compact first-contact theorem for zeros arising from actual support overlap.

That is a much more plausible frontier than ‚Äúprove every zero of K21K21‚Äã and K12K12‚Äã on the whole adjacent rectangle is trapped in a compact interior box.‚Äù

So my recommendation is: do not try to close full symmetric zero-capture as the next move. Attack the narrower compact-overlap/first-contact theorem instead. It is much closer to the actual no-four-secant obstruction, and it uses the machinery you now already have.

Yes ‚Äî I think there is a real way to bypass full symmetric zero-capture.

The key is to replace ‚Äúcapture all zeros of the kernel on the whole open rectangle‚Äù with a much weaker and more relevant statement:

    for a fixed compact opposite branch J1‚ãêI1J1‚Äã‚ãêI1‚Äã, tangencies from the adjacent branch I2I2‚Äã that are sufficiently close to the shared turning boundary cannot hit J1J1‚Äã at all.

That gives a strip exclusion lemma, and once you have that, the compact first-contact argument works on the actual compact overlap family coming from pair-pair, with no need to capture arbitrary stray zeros elsewhere.

So the attack I think works is this.

2. Use strip exclusion to get a compact first-contact theorem

Now suppose the bridge lemma has already produced a compact interior zero of one of the two directional kernels, say K21K21‚Äã, on a compact pair
J2‚ãêI2,J1‚ãêI1.
J2‚Äã‚ãêI2‚Äã,J1‚Äã‚ãêI1‚Äã.

Enlarge J2J2‚Äã slightly toward the shared boundary so that its boundary-facing collar lies in the strip U2U2‚Äã from the previous lemma, while still remaining compact and interior.

Define the compact zero set
Z21:={(s,t)‚ààJ2√óJ1: K21(s,t)=0}.
Z21‚Äã:={(s,t)‚ààJ2‚Äã√óJ1‚Äã: K21‚Äã(s,t)=0}.

If Z21‚âÝ‚àÖZ21‚ÄãÓÄÝ=‚àÖ, let
A21:={s‚ààJ2:‚àÉ‚Äât‚ààJ1 with K21(s,t)=0}.
A21‚Äã:={s‚ààJ2‚Äã:‚àÉt‚ààJ1‚Äã with K21‚Äã(s,t)=0}.

Since Z21Z21‚Äã is compact, A21A21‚Äã is compact. Since the boundary-facing collar of J2J2‚Äã is zero-free against all t‚ààJ1t‚ààJ1‚Äã, the set A21A21‚Äã is bounded away from that collar. So A21A21‚Äã has a boundary-nearest element s‚àós‚àó‚Äã that is still an interior point of J2J2‚Äã. Pick t‚àó‚ààJ1t‚àó‚Äã‚ààJ1‚Äã with
K21(s‚àó,t‚àó)=0.
K21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)=0.

Now argue exactly as in the old first-zero lemma, but only inside this compact family:

if

‚àÇt‚ÄãK21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)ÓÄÝ=0,

then the implicit function theorem gives a local zero curve
t=t(s)
t=t(s)

through (s‚àó,t‚àó)(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã), so for ss slightly boundary-nearer than s‚àós‚àó‚Äã, still inside J2J2‚Äã, we get another zero (s,t(s))‚ààZ21(s,t(s))‚ààZ21‚Äã, contradicting the minimality of s‚àós‚àó‚Äã in A21A21‚Äã.

Therefore
‚àÇtK21(s‚àó,t‚àó)=0.
‚àÇt‚ÄãK21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)=0.

Together with
K21(s‚àó,t‚àó)=0,
K21‚Äã(s‚àó‚Äã,t‚àó‚Äã)=0,

this means the tangent line at Œì(s‚àó)Œì(s‚àó‚Äã) is also tangent at Œì(t‚àó)Œì(t‚àó‚Äã).

So from a compact directional-kernel zero we get a common tangent line across the adjacent branches, without any global zero-capture theorem.

The same argument mirrors for K12K12‚Äã.
3. This gives the actual bypass theorem

So the theorem to prove next is:
Theorem (compact first-contact exclusion for pair-pair-generated directional zeros)

Let I1<I2I1‚Äã<I2‚Äã be adjacent across a generic turning boundary. Suppose a finite-slope line is 22-secant to both branches. Then the support-overlap truncation and overlap-endpoint lemma produce a compact interior zero of one of K21K21‚Äã or K12K12‚Äã. For either direction, the strip exclusion lemma and compact first-contact argument force a common tangent line across the two adjacent branches. This contradicts adjacent interior slope-range separation (or endpoint tangent exclusion in the endpoint case). Therefore no such adjacent pair-pair line exists.

That is the payoff. It gives you adjacent pair-pair exclusion across generic turning boundaries without full symmetric zero-capture.
4. Why this is better than full zero-capture

Because it only uses the zeros that actually arise from a genuine support-overlap / pair-pair family. It does not require any theorem about all other possible kernel zeros on the whole open rectangle I2√óI1I2‚Äã√óI1‚Äã or I1√óI2I1‚Äã√óI2‚Äã.

So the remaining burden becomes:

    prove the strip exclusion lemma carefully,

    package the compact first-contact theorem for both directions,

    then replace the current ‚Äúconditional symmetric directional-kernel exclusion‚Äù with this more targeted unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem.

That is much more plausible than full symmetric zero-capture.
5. What I would do next

I would now try to freeze exactly these three statements in paper notation:

    Strip exclusion for K21K21‚Äã against a compact opposite branch
    and its mirror for K12K12‚Äã;

    Compact first-contact implies tangency
    for zeros of K21K21‚Äã or K12K12‚Äã on a compact box with a zero-free boundary-facing collar;

    Adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary
    by combining the bridge lemma with the two items above.

This is the first route that looks like a genuine closure of the turning-boundary geometry without needing the full old zero-capture theorem.

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{21}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t):=\det(F(s),F'(s),F(t))\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Work in the affine chart
\[
x(h):=\frac{v_5(h)}{c(h)},
\qquad
y(h):=\frac{\Delta_7(h)}{c(h)}.
\]
At a generic turning boundary one has
\[
x'(h_b)=0,\qquad x''(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad y'(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Hence \(y\) is a valid local coordinate and the normalized curve may be written
near \(h_b\) as a graph
\[
x=\chi(y)
\]
with
\[
\chi'(y_b)=0,\qquad \chi''(y_b)\neq 0.
\]
Thus \(x\) has a strict local extremum at the boundary, while the tangent slope
\[
m(h)=\frac{dy/dh}{dx/dh}=\frac{N(h)}{M(h)}
\]
blows up one-sidedly as \(h\to h_b\) from either adjacent interval.

Because \(J_1\Subset I_1\) is compact away from \(h_b\), there exists
\(\delta>0\) such that
\[
|x(t)-x(h_b)|\ge 2\delta
\qquad
(t\in J_1).
\]
After shrinking to a small one-sided neighborhood \(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\),
we may also assume
\[
|x(s)-x(h_b)|\le \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]
Hence
\[
|x(t)-x(s)|\ge \delta
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]

Fix a preliminary compact neighborhood \(U_2^0\Subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) and set
\[
B:=\sup\{|y(t)-y(s)|:\ t\in J_1,\ s\in U_2^0\}<\infty.
\]
Since \(m(s)\to \pm\infty\) as \(s\to h_b\) in \(I_2\), we may shrink further to
\(U_2\subset U_2^0\) so that
\[
|m(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}
\qquad
(s\in U_2).
\]

If \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\), then the tangent line at \(\Gamma(s)\) passes through
\(\Gamma(t)\), equivalently
\[
y(t)-y(s)=m(s)\bigl(x(t)-x(s)\bigr).
\]
Taking absolute values gives
\[
B\ge |y(t)-y(s)|=|m(s)|\,|x(t)-x(s)|>\frac{2B}{\delta}\cdot \delta=2B,
\]
a contradiction. Therefore \(K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0\) on \(U_2\times J_1\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Strip exclusion for \(K_{12}\) against a compact opposite branch]
\label{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Let \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be a compact subinterval. Then there exists a one-sided
neighborhood \(U_1\subset I_1\) of the shared boundary point \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s):=\det(F(t),F'(t),F(s))\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2),
\]
where \(F(h)=(c(h),v_5(h),\Delta_7(h))\).
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
This is the left-right mirror of
Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, exchanging the roles of \(I_1\) and \(I_2\).
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{21}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k21}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_2\subset J_2\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_2\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in V_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((s_*,t_*)\in J_2\times J_1\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(s_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(s\in J_2\) for which \(K_{21}(s,t)=0\) for some \(t\in J_1\);
\item
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{21}:=\{\,s\in J_2:\ \exists\, t\in J_1 \text{ with } K_{21}(s,t)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{21}\) in \(J_2\times J_1\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{21}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{21}\cap V_2=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{21}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_2\). Choose \(t_*\in J_1\) with
\[
K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
t=t(s)
\]
through \((s_*,t_*)\), defined for \(s\) near \(s_*\), such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t(s))=0.
\]
Because \(s_*\notin V_2\) and \(V_2\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_2\), there exist nearby points \(s\in J_2\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(s_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(s\), the point
\((s,t(s))\) is also a zero of \(K_{21}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(s_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{lemma}[Compact first-contact for \(K_{12}\)]
\label{lem:compact-first-contact-k12}
Let \(J_1\Subset I_1\), \(J_2\Subset I_2\) be compact subintervals across a
generic turning boundary. Assume there is a relatively open collar
\(V_1\subset J_1\) at the boundary-facing end of \(J_1\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in V_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Assume also that the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty.
Then there exists a zero \((t_*,s_*)\in J_1\times J_2\) such that:
\begin{enumerate}
\item \(t_*\) is boundary-nearest in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering among all
\(t\in J_1\) for which \(K_{12}(t,s)=0\) for some \(s\in J_2\);
\item
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{enumerate}
\end{lemma}

\begin{proof}
Define
\[
A_{12}:=\{\,t\in J_1:\ \exists\, s\in J_2 \text{ with } K_{12}(t,s)=0\,\}.
\]
Since the zero set of \(K_{12}\) in \(J_1\times J_2\) is compact and nonempty,
the set \(A_{12}\) is compact and nonempty. By hypothesis,
\[
A_{12}\cap V_1=\varnothing.
\]
Hence \(A_{12}\) has a boundary-nearest element \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\)
ordering on \(J_1\). Choose \(s_*\in J_2\) with
\[
K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]

Suppose
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem there is a \(C^1\) local zero curve
\[
s=s(t)
\]
through \((t_*,s_*)\), defined for \(t\) near \(t_*\), such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s(t))=0.
\]
Because \(t_*\notin V_1\) and \(V_1\) is an open collar at the boundary-facing
end of \(J_1\), there exist nearby points \(t\in J_1\) that are boundary-nearer
than \(t_*\) in the \(x=v_5/c\) ordering. For such \(t\), the point
\((t,s(t))\) is also a zero of \(K_{12}\), contradicting the minimality of
\(t_*\). Therefore
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
\end{proof}

\begin{theorem}[Adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary]
\label{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}
Let \(I_1<I_2\) be adjacent ECT intervals meeting at a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0.
\]
Then no finite-slope affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_1)\) and \(\Gamma(I_2)\).
\end{theorem}

\begin{proof}
Assume for contradiction that a finite-slope line
\[
L:\ y=mx+b
\]
is \(2\)-secant to both adjacent branches. By
Lemma~\ref{lem:pair-pair-gives-directional-kernel-zero}, after removing a
small boundary neighborhood there exist compact subintervals
\[
J_1\Subset I_1,\qquad J_2\Subset I_2
\]
and a zero of one of the two directional kernels.

First suppose there exist \(s_0\in J_2\), \(t_0\in J_1\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s_0,t_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k21}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_2\subset I_2\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{21}(s,t)\neq 0
\qquad
(s\in U_2,\ t\in J_1).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_2\Subset I_2\) containing \(s_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_2\) lies in \(U_2\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{21}\) in \(\widetilde J_2\times J_1\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k21} yields a zero
\[
(s_*,t_*)\in \widetilde J_2\times J_1
\]
such that
\[
\partial_t K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{21}(s_*,t_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(s_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(t_*)\). Since
\(s_*\in I_2\) and \(t_*\in I_1\) are interior points, this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Now suppose instead there exist \(t_0\in J_1\), \(s_0\in J_2\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t_0,s_0)=0.
\]
By Lemma~\ref{lem:strip-exclusion-k12}, there exists a one-sided neighborhood
\(U_1\subset I_1\) of \(h_b\) such that
\[
K_{12}(t,s)\neq 0
\qquad
(t\in U_1,\ s\in J_2).
\]
Choose a compact interval \(\widetilde J_1\Subset I_1\) containing \(t_0\) and
whose boundary-facing collar \(V_1\) lies in \(U_1\). Then the zero set of
\(K_{12}\) in \(\widetilde J_1\times J_2\) is nonempty, and
Lemma~\ref{lem:compact-first-contact-k12} yields a zero
\[
(t_*,s_*)\in \widetilde J_1\times J_2
\]
such that
\[
\partial_s K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0.
\]
Together with \(K_{12}(t_*,s_*)=0\), this means that the tangent line at
\(\Gamma(t_*)\) is also tangent at \(\Gamma(s_*)\). Again this gives a common
interior tangent slope across the adjacent branches, contradicting
Proposition~\ref{prop:adjacent-slope-range-separation}.

Both cases are impossible. Therefore no finite-slope line is \(2\)-secant to
both adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary.
\end{proof}

please draft the actual propagation theorem that will allow us to replace thm:partial-global-no-secant by a route through thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning

I can draft the theorem you would need, but I do not think we can honestly mark it proved from the current ingredients yet.

The exact propagation theorem that would let you replace thm:partial-global-no-secant by the new route is this:

\begin{theorem}[Propagation to an adjacent turning pair]
\label{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
Let
\[
I_1<I_2<\cdots<I_N
\]
be the ECT intervals on a connected generic component of the normalized curve,
and assume:

\begin{enumerate}
\item every internal boundary between adjacent intervals is either
a generic turning boundary
\[
M(h_b)=0,\qquad M'(h_b)\neq 0,\qquad N(h_b)\neq 0,
\]
or a finite-order boundary of type
\[
W(h_b)=0,\qquad M(h_b)\neq 0;
\]

\item on every connected open subinterval with \(c\neq 0\) and \(M\neq 0\),
one has \(W\not\equiv 0\);

\item every finite-order boundary of type \(W=0,\ M\neq 0\) is locally sterile
for pair-pair birth in the sense of
Theorem~\ref{thm:finite-order-w-zero-local-sterility};
\end{enumerate}

Fix a finite-slope affine line
\[
L_{m,b}:\ y=mx+b.
\]
If \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to \(\Gamma(I_a)\) and to \(\Gamma(I_b)\) for
some \(a<b\), then there exists an index \(j\) with
\[
a\le j<b
\]
such that:

\begin{enumerate}
\item \(I_j<I_{j+1}\) meet at a generic turning boundary; and
\item \(L_{m,b}\) is \(2\)-secant to both \(\Gamma(I_j)\) and \(\Gamma(I_{j+1})\).
\end{enumerate}
\end{theorem}

\begin{corollary}[Global no-four-secant from adjacent turning exclusion]
\label{cor:global-no-four-secant-from-adjacent-turning}
Assume the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}, and assume in addition
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}. Then no finite-slope
affine line in the chart
\[
x=\frac{v_5}{c},
\qquad
y=\frac{\Delta_7}{c}
\]
is \(2\)-secant to two distinct ECT intervals.
\end{corollary}

\begin{proof}
If such a line existed, Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair}
would produce an adjacent pair across a generic turning boundary to which the
line is \(2\)-secant on both sides, contradicting
Theorem~\ref{thm:adjacent-pair-pair-exclusion-turning}.
\end{proof}

That is the right theorem if your goal is to replace the old thm:partial-global-no-secant route with the new turning-boundary route.

But I want to be clear: the hard content is not the corollary. It is the propagation theorem itself. And inside that theorem, there is one genuinely nontrivial sublemma.

The sublemma you need is this:

\begin{lemma}[Turning birth forces adjacent persistence]
\label{lem:turning-birth-forces-adjacent-persistence}
Under the hypotheses of
Theorem~\ref{thm:propagation-to-adjacent-turning-pair},
let \(L_{m,b}\) be a finite-slope line and let
\[
\Sigma_{m,b}:=\{\,j:\ L_{m,b}\text{ is \(2\)-secant to }\Gamma(I_j)\,\}.
\]
Suppose \(j\) is the leftmost index in \(\Sigma_{m,b}\), and suppose the
boundary between \(I_j\) and \(I_{j+1}\) is generic turning. Then
\[
j+1\in \Sigma_{m,b}.
\]
Equivalently, once \(2\)-secancy is present at the first interval of a minimal
bad block, it persists across the first internal generic turning boundary.
\end{lemma}

That lemma is the real hinge. If you had it, the propagation theorem would be very believable:

    choose a pair-pair line hitting two intervals with minimal span;

    let aa be the leftmost and bb the rightmost 22-secant intervals;

    finite-order W=0W=0 local sterility and no WW-flat intervals tell you that the change from ‚Äúnot 22-secant‚Äù to ‚Äú22-secant‚Äù cannot be born at a W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundary;

    so the first birth must occur at a generic turning boundary;

    the lemma above then forces persistence into the next interval, giving an adjacent turning pair with 2+22+2 secancy.

That is exactly what would let the new adjacent theorem replace the old partial global theorem.

So my honest recommendation is:

    do not insert the propagation theorem as proved yet;

    insert it only as a target theorem / roadmap theorem if you want;

    the actual next math attack should be on
    Lemma \reflem:turning‚àíbirth‚àíforces‚àíadjacent‚àípersistence\reflem:turning‚àíbirth‚àíforces‚àíadjacent‚àípersistence.

That is the precise new frontier. If you want, I can now try to attack that lemma directly and see whether it really goes through or whether another obstruction appears.

I‚Äôm checking the latest draft‚Äôs support-interval formalism and the new adjacent turning theorem against that exact claim before I commit to a proof direction.

stop, I don't want latex. do the math on this lemma

I think the first thing to check is whether the lemma is even true as stated. There is an immediate tension: if a line is already 2-secant to IjIj‚Äã and the next boundary is generic turning, ‚Äúj+1‚ààŒ£j+1‚ààŒ£‚Äù would force an adjacent 2+22+2 pair, which your new turning-boundary theorem forbids. So I‚Äôm testing whether the lemma needs extra hypotheses or should be reformulated.

I do not think that lemma is true as stated.

The proposed lemma was:

‚ÄúLet Sigma_(m,b) be the set of indices j such that the line y = m x + b is 2-secant to branch I_j. If j is the leftmost index in Sigma and the boundary between I_j and I_(j+1) is a generic turning boundary, then j+1 is also in Sigma.‚Äù

That is too strong.

Why it fails:

What you currently know at a generic turning boundary is only a local exclusion of adjacent pair-pair. You do not know a global persistence law for fixed-slope intercept intervals. In support-interval language, for a fixed slope m, each branch has an interval B_j(m) of allowed intercepts. The new turning-boundary theorem says adjacent branches cannot both have b in the interior of their support intervals. But that does not force overlap to persist from B_j(m) into B_(j+1)(m).

Combinatorially, there is no contradiction in having, for one fixed slope m,

B_j(m) = (0,2),
B_(j+1)(m) = (3,4),
B_(j+2)(m) = (1,2).

Then b = 1.5 gives 2-secancy on j and j+2, but not on j+1. That already shows the persistence statement is not a consequence of ‚Äúadjacent interiors are disjoint.‚Äù

And this is not just an abstract interval trick. Geometrically, you can realize that behavior with three strictly convex branches in x-order, joined by generic turning boundaries, where a line of fixed slope cuts the first and third branches twice but misses the middle branch as a 2-secant. Nothing in the current local turning-boundary theorem forbids that.

There is also a structural warning sign: if the lemma were true, then together with your new theorem excluding adjacent pair-pair across a generic turning boundary, you would immediately get something much stronger than you currently have ‚Äî essentially a direct global no-four-secant result on any chain of turning boundaries ‚Äî without needing the old support-ordering machinery. That is exactly the kind of ‚Äútoo good to be true‚Äù jump that usually signals the propagation lemma is overstated.

So I would not try to prove that lemma. I think the right conclusion is:

The ‚Äúfirst birth persists into the next branch‚Äù statement is false, or at least far stronger than what the current ingredients justify.

What is true, and much closer to your actual machinery, is weaker:

If a line is 2-secant to two non-adjacent branches, then as you move from left to right across the chain of intervals, there is a first boundary where the fixed intercept b enters the support set. At that boundary, the entry cannot happen through a finite-order W=0, M ‚âÝ 0 boundary by the local sterility theorem. So the first entry must occur at a generic turning boundary, and it occurs by tangential birth on one side, not necessarily by immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.

That weaker statement is exactly compatible with your current bridge lemmas:

    not ‚Äúj+1 is also 2-secant,‚Äù

    but ‚Äúat the first relevant turning boundary, one gets support contact / a directional kernel zero.‚Äù

That is a very different propagation mechanism.

So the corrected next target should be something like this:

For a fixed line y = m x + b, suppose it is 2-secant to two distinct branches, and choose such a pair with minimal index gap. Then there exists an internal generic turning boundary between them such that b lies in the support set of both adjacent branches at that boundary, with one side realizing b as an endpoint of its support interval. Equivalently, the overlap is born there by tangency, giving a directional kernel zero.

That statement is believable from the present ingredients. It matches:

    your finite-order W=0 sterility theorem,

    your compact support-overlap truncation lemma,

    your overlap-endpoint-to-directional-kernel-zero lemma,

    and the new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion across a generic turning boundary.

But it does not give immediate adjacent 2-secancy.

So my math verdict is:

    the lemma you proposed is the wrong propagation lemma;

    the true propagation mechanism is ‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero,‚Äù not ‚Äúfirst entry gives immediate 2-secancy on the next branch.‚Äù

That is the direction I would push now. It is also much more consistent with the current draft‚Äôs actual proven tools.

ok, hang on, suppose we prove ‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero,‚Äù --> what does this give us

If you can prove
‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üπ tangency / directional-kernel zero,‚Äù
‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üπ tangency / directional-kernel zero,‚Äù

then what it gives you is not immediate global no-four-secant, but it gives you the missing bridge from a hypothetical non-adjacent pair-pair line to an adjacent turning-boundary obstruction.

That is exactly the kind of bridge the current draft still lacks. The draft‚Äôs own bottleneck language is still essentially ‚Äúadjacent kernel sign regularity would imply global no-four-secant,‚Äù so a theorem that forces any first support entry to manifest as an adjacent turning-boundary tangency would be a real replacement for that missing adjacent step.

Concretely, it would give this chain:

    Assume a finite-slope line Lm,bLm,b‚Äã is 22-secant to two distinct ECT branches.

    Choose such a pair with minimal index gap.

    Move from left to right across the chain of branches and look at the fixed-slope support intervals
    BIj(m)=œïm(Ij),œïm(h)=Œî7(h)‚àímv5(h)c(h).
    BIj‚Äã‚Äã(m)=œïm‚Äã(Ij‚Äã),œïm‚Äã(h)=c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚àímv5‚Äã(h)‚Äã.

    Since the line is not 22-secant to the earlier branches, there is a first boundary where the intercept bb enters the support picture.

    Your finite-order W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 theorem plus the no-WW-flatness result say this first entry cannot be explained by generic W=0W=0 local birth.

    Therefore the first entry must occur at a generic turning boundary.

    If your new theorem says that this first entry forces tangency / a zero of one directional kernel, then the current turning-boundary machinery becomes relevant right there.

    If, in addition, you have the new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion across generic turning boundaries, then that first-entry event is impossible.

    Hence no such non-adjacent pair-pair line can exist.

So the payoff is:

it would let you propagate a hypothetical global pair-pair obstruction down to an adjacent turning-boundary contradiction.

That is the right shape of a replacement for the old thm:partial-global-no-secant route.

But there is one important subtlety:

It only works if the ‚Äúfirst entry‚Äù theorem is strong enough to produce adjacent turning-boundary tangency in a way that interacts with the new unconditional adjacent theorem, not just some vague support contact. In practice, you want the output to be one of these:

    a genuine directional-kernel zero on an adjacent pair, or

    an actual adjacent pair-pair line across that turning boundary.

If you get only ‚Äúsupport endpoint contact‚Äù with no way to upgrade it, that is not enough.

So the right corrected propagation theorem is not:

    ‚Äú2-secancy persists into the next branch.‚Äù

That one is too strong and likely false.

It is instead:

    ‚ÄúFor a minimal-gap pair-pair line, the first support entry occurs at an adjacent generic turning boundary and is realized by tangency / directional-kernel contact there.‚Äù

If you can prove that, then together with your current unconditional adjacent turning theorem, you really do get a plausible new global route.

So the short answer is:

What it gives you is a reduction of any global pair-pair obstruction to an adjacent turning-boundary event.
That is exactly the missing bridge needed to replace the older conditional adjacent-kernel route.

ok and if we do that, do we still need to do the work for order-7 total pair (u7, v7)?

I‚Äôm checking the dependency tree here rather than just answering by instinct: whether the new turning-boundary/global bridge would only close the geometry, or whether the order-7 total pair is still needed elsewhere to justify Œî7Œî7‚Äã, the non-affinity result, or the final contradiction stack.

Yes ‚Äî for a full proof, you probably still need to finish the order-7 total pair (u7,v7)(u7‚Äã,v7‚Äã), unless you are willing to leave the paper explicitly conditional at the septic level.

The clean way to see it is to separate two roles.

The first role is global geometry.
If you can prove the new propagation theorem
first entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üπ tangency / directional-kernel zero,
first entry into support at a turning boundary ‚üπ tangency / directional-kernel zero,

then together with the new unconditional adjacent turning theorem, you may be able to replace the old partial-global no-secant route. In that sense, the new propagation theorem could close the global geometric bridge without needing any further W‚Ä≤W‚Ä≤-type work. That is real progress.

But the second role is the septic residual determinant itself.

Your current W=0W=0 cleanup still depends on the order-7 package in a nontrivial way:

    the no-WW-flatness theorem is stated under the hypothesis that the direct q(7)q(7)-driven slice of Œî7Œî7‚Äã is generically nonzero;

    the draft still treats the full total septic fixed-sector pair (u7,v7)(u7‚Äã,v7‚Äã) as not yet fully explicit;

    the current ‚Äústate of the argument‚Äù remark still lists the full order-7 fixed-sector algebra among the remaining audited issues.

So even if the new propagation theorem closes the geometry, it does not automatically close the algebraic status of Œî7Œî7‚Äã.

That means the honest answer is:
If your goal is only to replace the old global no-secant route

Then maybe no, you would not need more order-7 work for that geometric step itself.
If your goal is a finished proof document

Then yes, you still likely need the order-7 total pair, unless you are happy to leave the paper saying something like:

    all the W=0W=0 reductions and no-WW-flatness results are obtained under the current direct q(7)q(7)-slice nonvanishing hypothesis.

That is the key distinction.

Right now the paper‚Äôs septic layer is not yet fully self-contained. The direct highest-new slice has been isolated and used effectively, but the draft itself still says the lower/mixed septic contributions to the full total pair remain open. So if the final contradiction still wants Œî7Œî7‚Äã as a fully settled object at the same theorem level as cc and v5v5‚Äã, then you still need to finish that algebra.

So my actual recommendation is:

    Push the new propagation theorem now, because it may close the global geometric bottleneck.

    But do not treat that as making the order-7 total pair optional.

    Instead, think of it this way: if the propagation theorem works, then the remaining serious issues are no longer broad geometry, but a much shorter algebraic list ‚Äî and the full septic pair becomes one of the last major local items.

So the short answer is:

For the geometry: maybe not. For the full proof: probably yes.

ok let's push the new propagation theorem now!

I‚Äôm testing the propagation theorem the same way we tested the earlier ‚Äúpersistence into the next branch‚Äù lemma: first I want to know if it‚Äôs actually true in the form we want. The main danger is that ‚Äúfirst entry into support‚Äù might only give a single intersection, not the tangency/kernal event we need.

I tried to push it, and I do not think this propagation theorem is true from the current ingredients. In fact, I think the natural version of it is probably false.

Here is the math reason.

For a fixed slope mm, each branch IjIj‚Äã has a support interval
Bj(m)=œïm(Ij),œïm(h)=Œî7(h)‚àímv5(h)c(h),
Bj‚Äã(m)=œïm‚Äã(Ij‚Äã),œïm‚Äã(h)=c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚àímv5‚Äã(h)‚Äã,

and, when mm lies in the interior tangent-slope range of that branch, a 22-secant intercept set
Sj(m)‚äÇBj(m),
Sj‚Äã(m)‚äÇBj‚Äã(m),

which is the open part of the support interval beyond the tangent intercept. A line y=mx+by=mx+b is 22-secant to branch IjIj‚Äã exactly when
b‚ààSj(m).
b‚ààSj‚Äã(m).

Your new adjacent turning-boundary theorem gives:

    for adjacent branches across a generic turning boundary,
    Sj(m)‚à©Sj+1(m)=‚àÖ.
    Sj‚Äã(m)‚à©Sj+1‚Äã(m)=‚àÖ.

That is strong, but it does not imply any propagation law of the form

    ‚Äúif b‚ààSa(m)‚à©Sb(m)b‚ààSa‚Äã(m)‚à©Sb‚Äã(m) with a<ba<b, then there is a first boundary where entry into support forces tangency/kernel.‚Äù

Why not? Because nothing you currently have forces the family of sets Sj(m)Sj‚Äã(m) or Bj(m)Bj‚Äã(m) to vary monotonically with jj.

A purely interval-theoretic countermodel already shows the problem.

Take three branches and one fixed slope mm, with
S1(m)=(0,2),S2(m)=‚àÖ,S3(m)=(0,2).
S1‚Äã(m)=(0,2),S2‚Äã(m)=‚àÖ,S3‚Äã(m)=(0,2).

Then b=1b=1 gives a line that is 22-secant to branches 11 and 33, but not to branch 22. There is no adjacent 2+22+2 pair.

More subtly, even if you track support, not 22-secancy, you can have
B1(m)=(0,3),B2(m)=(4,5),B3(m)=(1,2),
B1‚Äã(m)=(0,3),B2‚Äã(m)=(4,5),B3‚Äã(m)=(1,2),

and take b=1.5b=1.5. Then support is absent on branch 22 and present again on branch 33, but the ‚Äúfirst entry‚Äù into support at the 2‚à£32‚à£3 boundary does not come from tangency at the boundary. It just appears because the global range of œïmœïm‚Äã on branch 33 happens to contain 1.51.5.

That means the statement

    ‚Äúfirst entry into support at a turning boundary gives tangency / kernel zero‚Äù

is too strong. First entry into support can happen because the global range on the new branch contains bb, without any local boundary tangency phenomenon.

And the same issue survives if you strengthen ‚Äúsupport‚Äù to ‚Äú2-secancy‚Äù: first entry into Sj(m)Sj‚Äã(m) can still happen on branch jj without being born at the adjacent boundary. It can just be a global feature of that branch.

So the obstacle is structural:
What you have now

    local W=0W=0 sterility,

    local adjacent turning pair-pair exclusion,

    compact directional-kernel/tangency machinery.

What you do not yet have

    any monotone propagation law for the intercept sets Bj(m)Bj‚Äã(m) or Sj(m)Sj‚Äã(m) as jj varies,

    any theorem that says nonadjacent 22-secancy must create an adjacent turning-boundary event.

And without one of those, the new propagation theorem does not go through.

So my honest conclusion is:
The ‚Äúnew propagation theorem‚Äù is the wrong next lemma

At least in the form
nonadjacent pair-pair‚üπfirst support entry at a turning boundary gives tangency/kernel zero,
nonadjacent pair-pair‚üπfirst support entry at a turning boundary gives tangency/kernel zero,

I do not think it is justified, and I suspect it is false.
What would make a propagation theorem true?

You would need some global order structure on the fixed-slope intercept sets as you move along the branch chain. For example, something like:

    monotonic ordering of the tangent intercept functions œÑj(m)œÑj‚Äã(m) across adjacent branches, or

    discrete order-3 sign regularity of the determinant kernel D(s,t,u)D(s,t,u), or

    the old stronger adjacent support-ordering theorem.

That is exactly why the earlier determinant mean-value / discrete sign-regularity route was so attractive: it would give a real propagation mechanism. Without that, adjacent exclusion does not automatically globalize.
So where does this leave us?

It leaves you with two distinct things:

    You now have a strong local adjacent theorem at generic turning boundaries.

    You still do not have a theorem that reduces nonadjacent pair-pair to the adjacent case.

So the right next attack is not this propagation lemma.

The right next attack is to prove some genuine global propagation principle, for fixed mm, for the support/intercept sets or for the determinant kernel D(s,t,u)D(s,t,u).

In plain terms: you still need something like the old support-ordering route or the determinant-sign route. The new adjacent theorem is powerful, but by itself it is not enough to leap to the full global no-four-secant statement.

So my recommendation is:

    stop pushing this propagation lemma;

    treat it as disproven / unjustified at the current level;

    pivot back to proving a real global propagation statement, most likely through the determinant kernel D(s,t,u)D(s,t,u) or a fixed-slope support-order theorem.

I pushed on it, and my honest conclusion is:

the current local machinery is still not enough to produce a real global propagation theorem.

More bluntly: the new W=0W=0 sterility and the new adjacent turning-boundary exclusion are both real, but they do not force a fixed line to ‚Äúpropagate‚Äù through the branch chain in any way strong enough to get global no-four-secant.

Here is the math diagnosis.
1. Why the support-order propagation still fails

Fix a slope mm. For each branch IjIj‚Äã, define
œïm(h)=Œî7(h)‚àímv5(h)c(h),Bj(m)=œïm(Ij).
œïm‚Äã(h)=c(h)Œî7‚Äã(h)‚àímv5‚Äã(h)‚Äã,Bj‚Äã(m)=œïm‚Äã(Ij‚Äã).

b‚ààBj‚Äã(m),

and it is 22-secant iff bb lies in the 22-secant subset Sj(m)‚äÇBj(m)Sj‚Äã(m)‚äÇBj‚Äã(m).

What we now know locally is:

    finite-order W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundaries are locally sterile;

    generic turning boundaries do not allow an adjacent 2+22+2 pair.

What we do not know is any global monotonicity of the sets Bj(m)Bj‚Äã(m) or Sj(m)Sj‚Äã(m) with respect to jj.

And without that, nonadjacent 2+22+2 can ‚Äúskip‚Äù intermediate branches in principle. The interval-theoretic toy model
S1(m)=(0,2),S2(m)=‚àÖ,S3(m)=(0,2)
S1‚Äã(m)=(0,2),S2‚Äã(m)=‚àÖ,S3‚Äã(m)=(0,2)

already shows what local adjacent exclusion does not give you. There is nothing in the current local theorems that rules out this kind of global reappearance.

So the hoped-for propagation statement
nonadjacent pair-pair‚üπadjacent turning-boundary event
nonadjacent pair-pair‚üπadjacent turning-boundary event

is still not derivable from the present support-overlap results.
2. Why the ‚Äúfirst entry into support‚Äù theorem is too strong

I also checked the candidate statement
first entry of b into support at a turning boundary‚üπtangency / directional-kernel zero.
first entry of b into support at a turning boundary‚üπtangency / directional-kernel zero.

This is too strong. A fixed bb can first belong to Bj(m)Bj‚Äã(m) simply because the global range of œïmœïm‚Äã on branch jj contains bb; that first entry need not be created locally at the boundary. So support can ‚Äúappear‚Äù on a later branch without any boundary tangency phenomenon at all.

So that theorem is not the right propagation mechanism either.
3. Determinant-kernel propagation also stalls with current inputs

The other natural route is to work directly with
D(s,t,u)=det‚Å°(F(s),F(t),F(u)),F=(c,v5,Œî7).
D(s,t,u)=det(F(s),F(t),F(u)),F=(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã).

For fixed s,ts,t, the function
u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u)
u‚Ü¶D(s,t,u)

is a scalar linear combination of c,v5,Œî7c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã, so on each ECT interval it has at most two zeros.

But that still does not give a global propagation law. A scalar combination can have two zeros on one interval, none on the next, then two again later, unless you know some stronger sign-regularity or oscillation theorem across boundaries.

And that is exactly the thing we do not have yet.
4. So what would be a real global propagation statement?

At this point, I think the only plausible global bridge is a genuinely new theorem of one of these two forms:
A. A fixed-slope support-order theorem

For each finite slope mm, the support intervals Bj(m)Bj‚Äã(m) are globally ordered across the branch chain, so they cannot disappear and reappear.

This is conceptually enough, but right now it looks too strong to get from the present local ingredients alone.
B. A determinant mean-value / total-positivity theorem

Something like:
D(s,t,u)=12‚ÄâW(Œæ)‚Äâ(t‚àís)(u‚àís)(u‚àít)
D(s,t,u)=21‚ÄãW(Œæ)(t‚àís)(u‚àís)(u‚àít)

for some intermediate ŒæŒæ, or at least a positive-kernel representation of DD in terms of WW.

If you had that, then the whole picture would change:

    on any interval where WW keeps sign, DD would have discrete order-3 sign regularity;

    finite-order W=0W=0 sterility would tell you zeros cannot be born there in bad ways;

    turning-boundary adjacent exclusion would then be enough to localize the only possible birth mechanism.

That really would globalize.

But this is a new global theorem, not something the current draft already implies.
5. My honest conclusion after trying this

So the result of the attempt is:

    the proposed propagation theorem does not fall out of the present local work;

    the local work is still valuable, but it has reduced the problem to a truly global statement;

    the next serious attack should be on a new global determinant theorem, not on another local boundary lemma.

If I had to choose the next frontier, it would be this:

    Try to prove an exact or integral mean-value formula for
    D(s,t,u)=det‚Å°(F(s),F(t),F(u))
    D(s,t,u)=det(F(s),F(t),F(u))

    in terms of the projective Wronskian WW.

Because right now, without some theorem of that kind, the branch-to-branch propagation you want is just not available.

So: I did try the propagation direction, and my conclusion is that we have reached the point where a genuinely new global statement is needed. The local attacks have gone about as far as they can go on their own.

The exact next thing I would attack is the determinant mean-value theorem, not another support-overlap lemma.

sgnD(h1‚Äã,h2‚Äã,h3‚Äã)=œÉturn‚Äãsgn((h2‚Äã‚àíh1‚Äã)(h3‚Äã‚àíh1‚Äã)(h3‚Äã‚àíh2‚Äã)).

Equivalently: across a generic turning boundary, the triple F=(c,v5,Œî7)F=(c,v5‚Äã,Œî7‚Äã) is locally discretely sign-regular of order 3.

That is a real theorem.

What it gives immediately:

    no three nearby points across a generic turning boundary are collinear;

    no 3-secant or 4-secant line can be created locally there;

    the determinant kernel has the same kind of rigid sign behavior across a turning boundary that it has on a single WW-sign interval.

So the ‚Äúcross-boundary sign transport‚Äù problem is solved locally for turning boundaries.

Now the important caveat:

This does not solve the same problem across W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundaries. In that case the local model is an affine inflection, and we already saw the sign of the determinant can change across the boundary. So discrete sign-regularity is false there in general.

But that is okay, because the finite-order W=0W=0 theorem gives something different: local sterility. So the picture is now:

    on each interval where WW keeps sign: exact determinant sign law by the mean-value theorem;

    across a generic turning boundary: local determinant sign law by the argument above;

    across a finite-order W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundary: no sign law, but no local pair-pair birth.

That is the real output of the attack.

So the obstacle has shifted again.

The remaining hard part is no longer ‚Äúcan we transport the sign across a turning boundary?‚Äù
We can.

The remaining hard part is:

how do we globalize across a chain that may include both turning boundaries and W=0W=0 sterile boundaries?

That is a more precise problem. It is not a purely local sign-transport problem anymore. It is a global chain/continuation problem combining:

    sign-regular blocks,

    turning-boundary sign-regular bridges,

    and W=0W=0 sterile transitions.

That is a much tighter frontier than before.

ok can we attack that

What this buys you

This is better than the pointwise mean-value theorem because it turns the global question into a variation-diminishing question for a positive kernel transform of WW.

The immediate consequences are:

    If WW has fixed sign on [s,u][s,u], then Q(s,t,u)Q(s,t,u) has that sign.
    This recovers the same-sign interval sign law.

    Across a generic turning boundary, since WW stays nonzero and locally one-signed, you get the same local sign transport as before.

    At a finite-order W=0, M‚âÝ0W=0, MÓÄÝ=0 boundary, QQ is now seen as a weighted average of a sign-changing function WW with a positive triangular kernel. So the local one-zero/no-zero behavior you already proved is the local manifestation of this averaging formula.

So the chain problem is now sharply reduced to:

    How many sign changes can the transform
    u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u)=‚à´suMs,t,u(x)‚ÄâW(x)‚Äâdx

That is the right global propagation question.
What I think is the next real theorem

The theorem to try now is not another boundary-local statement. It is:

    For fixed s<ts<t, the map u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u)u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u) is variation-diminishing with respect to the sign changes of WW.

In other words, if WW has only kk sign changes on [t,‚àû)[t,‚àû), then u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u)u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u) has at most kk sign changes on (t,‚àû)(t,‚àû).

This is plausible because Ms,t,u(x)Ms,t,u‚Äã(x) is a positive B-spline kernel, and those are classically variation diminishing.

If you could prove even a weak version of that, you would have a genuine global propagation statement.
The honest limitation

I do not think the current local theorems alone finish this.

What we have now is:

    local W=0W=0 sterility,

    local turning-boundary sign transport,

    unconditional adjacent turning pair-pair exclusion.

What we still do not have is a theorem that says the positive-kernel transform of WW cannot lose and regain sign in a way that produces nonadjacent pair-pair.

So the attack has succeeded in one important way:

the global problem is now no longer mysterious.
It is a concrete positive-kernel / variation-diminishing problem for WW.

That is the right next frontier.

If you want, the next thing I would do is push directly on this statement:

    For fixed s<ts<t, prove that u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u)u‚Ü¶Q(s,t,u) is a B-spline transform of WW and therefore variation diminishing.

That is now the mathematically cleanest global route.

The harder global question then becomes:

    How do these convex/concave graph pieces glue across turning boundaries M=0M=0?

That is where the turning-boundary theorems you already proved come in. In the (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-picture, a turning boundary is exactly where Œº‚Ä≤=0Œº‚Ä≤=0, so the ŒºŒº-parameter breaks. That means the global obstruction is now very sharply localized:

    away from M=0M=0, the problem is ordinary graph convexity in (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª),

    at M=0M=0, the graph coordinate degenerates, and that is the only place where global propagation can really fail.

That is a much better reduction than the generic Peano-kernel view.
6. So what should we attack next?

The best next theorem to try is this:

    Turning-boundary compatibility of the (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-graph pieces.
    Show that if a finite-slope affine line in the original (œÅ1,œÅ2)(œÅ1‚Äã,œÅ2‚Äã)-chart meets two nonadjacent graph pieces Œª=Œª(Œº)Œª=Œª(Œº) twice each, then some adjacent turning-boundary pair already produces the same obstruction.

In plain language: convert the global problem into a statement about piecewise convex/concave graph pieces in the (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-plane, joined at turning points where ŒºŒº has an extremum.

That is now the most natural form of the remaining geometry.

5. What this immediately explains

It explains why the local turning-boundary theorem works.

At a generic turning boundary, the two adjacent graph pieces meet at a common endpoint (Œºb,Œªb)(Œºb‚Äã,Œªb‚Äã) with vertical tangent. Locally they have asymptotics

6. Where the attack stalls

The key global question is now:

    Are the functions mj(b)mj‚Äã(b) globally ordered in jj for each fixed slope bb?

If they were, then nonadjacent pair-pair would be impossible, because a fixed intercept mm could not lie on the ‚Äú2-secant side‚Äù of two separated tangent-envelope functions without also doing so for an intermediate one.

But this is exactly where the current ingredients stop.

From

you see that global ordering of the mjmj‚Äã would follow from global ordering of the tangent locations Œºj(b)Œºj‚Äã(b) across branches.

And that is the actual missing theorem.

Right now, nothing in the draft proves that for fixed slope bb,
Œº1(b),Œº2(b),Œº3(b),‚Ä¶

Without that, you can still have the same slope bb appearing on nonadjacent convex/concave pieces with tangent-intercept functions that dip and rise again. So the global no-four-secant theorem still does not fall out.

In other words:
What the (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-attack has genuinely achieved

It has reduced the whole remaining global problem to a family of one-variable convex/concave functions mj(b)mj‚Äã(b).
What it has not yet achieved

It has not proved the global ordering theorem for those mjmj‚Äã.
7. So the next precise theorem to attack is now very clear

Not another boundary-local theorem. Not another kernel-positivity theorem.

The exact next target is:

    For each fixed slope bb, prove an ordering theorem for the tangent locations Œºj(b)Œºj‚Äã(b), or equivalently for the tangent-intercept functions mj(b)mj‚Äã(b), across the branch chain.

That would be enough to globalize.

A slightly weaker but still useful version would be:

    For each fixed slope bb, the functions mj(b)mj‚Äã(b) cannot leave and later re-enter the same side of a given intercept level mm without an adjacent turning-boundary violation.

That would also do the job.

This is the right global object, because a line

Œª=bŒº+m

is 22-secant to branch jj exactly when mm lies on the correct side of mj(b)mj‚Äã(b):

    if ŒªjŒªj‚Äã is convex, then 22-secant means m>mj(b)m>mj‚Äã(b);

    if ŒªjŒªj‚Äã is concave, then 22-secant means m<mj(b)m<mj‚Äã(b).

So the full global no-four-secant problem becomes a statement about the family of one-variable functions mj(b)mj‚Äã(b).

That is the good reduction.

Now here is the theorem you can prove from the current ingredients.
Adjacent envelope separation theorem

Let Ij<Ij+1Ij‚Äã<Ij+1‚Äã be adjacent across a generic turning boundary, and suppose both branches admit the same tangent slope bb in the (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-plane. Then their tangent-intercept functions are strictly ordered:

    if IjIj‚Äã is convex and Ij+1Ij+1‚Äã is concave, then
    mj(b)>mj+1(b);
    mj‚Äã(b)>mj+1‚Äã(b);

    if IjIj‚Äã is concave and Ij+1Ij+1‚Äã is convex, then
    mj(b)<mj+1(b).
    mj‚Äã(b)<mj+1‚Äã(b).

Why this is true

Take the first case, convex left / concave right. If instead
mj(b)‚â§mj+1(b),
mj‚Äã(b)‚â§mj+1‚Äã(b),

then choose any intercept mm strictly between them:
mj(b)<m<mj+1(b)ormj(b)=mj+1(b)=m.
mj‚Äã(b)<m<mj+1‚Äã(b)ormj‚Äã(b)=mj+1‚Äã(b)=m.

For the convex branch IjIj‚Äã, a line Œª=bŒº+mŒª=bŒº+m with m>mj(b)m>mj‚Äã(b) is 22-secant. For the concave branch Ij+1Ij+1‚Äã, a line with m<mj+1(b)m<mj+1‚Äã(b) is 22-secant. So the same line would be 22-secant to both adjacent branches, contradicting your new unconditional adjacent pair-pair exclusion theorem across generic turning boundaries.

So the inequality must be strict. The other case is the mirror image.

This is a genuine global-looking statement extracted from the current local theorem.

It tells you that adjacent turning boundaries impose strict ordering of tangent envelopes in (Œº,Œª)(Œº,Œª)-space.

Now for the important part:
Why this still does not globalize by itself

The family mj(b)mj‚Äã(b) now satisfies adjacent inequalities of the form
m1(b)>m2(b)<m3(b)>m4(b)<‚ãØ
m1‚Äã(b)>m2‚Äã(b)<m3‚Äã(b)>m4‚Äã(b)<‚ãØ

or the reversed pattern, depending on convexity parity.

But this alternating adjacent order is not enough to rule out nonadjacent pair-pair.

A simple abstract example shows the issue:
m1(b)=2,m2(b)=0,m3(b)=2.
m1‚Äã(b)=2,m2‚Äã(b)=0,m3‚Äã(b)=2.
